<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Arctic Ocean</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="hatnote" role="note">"Arctic Sea" redirects here. For the cargo ship, see <a href="/wiki/MV_Arctic_Sea" title="MV Arctic Sea">MV Arctic Sea</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arctic_Ocean_-_en.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="330" data-file-width="329" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Arctic_Ocean_-_en.png/250px-Arctic_Ocean_-_en.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/59/Arctic_Ocean_-_en.png 1.5x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arctic_Ocean_-_en.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of the Arctic Ocean, with borders as delineated by the <a href="/wiki/International_Hydrographic_Organization" title="International Hydrographic Organization">International Hydrographic Organization (IHO)</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Bay" title="Hudson Bay">Hudson Bay</a> (some of which is south of <a href="/wiki/57th_parallel_north" title="57th parallel north">57°N latitude</a>, off the map).</div>
</div>
</div>

</table>
<p>The <b>Arctic Ocean</b> is the smallest and shallowest of the world's five major <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pidwirny_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pidwirny-1">[1]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_Hydrographic_Organization" title="International Hydrographic Organization">International Hydrographic Organization</a> (IHO) recognizes it as an ocean, although some <a href="/wiki/Oceanography" title="Oceanography">oceanographers</a> call it the <b>Arctic Mediterranean Sea</b> or simply the <b>Arctic Sea</b>, classifying it a <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_sea_(oceanography)" title="Mediterranean sea (oceanography)">mediterranean sea</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Estuary" title="Estuary">estuary</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Alternatively, the Arctic Ocean can be seen as the northernmost part of the all-encompassing <a href="/wiki/World_Ocean" title="World Ocean">World Ocean</a>.</p>
<p>Located mostly in the <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a> <a href="/wiki/North_Pole" title="North Pole">north polar</a> region in the middle of the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a>, the Arctic Ocean is almost completely surrounded by <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>. It is partly covered by <a href="/wiki/Sea_ice" title="Sea ice">sea ice</a> throughout the year and almost completely in <a href="/wiki/Winter" title="Winter">winter</a>. The Arctic Ocean's <a href="/wiki/Sea_surface_temperature" title="Sea surface temperature">surface temperature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a> vary seasonally as the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_ice_pack" title="Arctic ice pack">ice cover</a> melts and freezes;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> its salinity is the lowest on average of the five major oceans, due to low <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a>, heavy <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">fresh water</a> inflow from rivers and streams, and limited connection and outflow to surrounding oceanic waters with higher salinities. The summer shrinking of the ice has been quoted at 50%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pidwirny_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pidwirny-1">[1]</a></sup> The US <a href="/wiki/National_Snow_and_Ice_Data_Center" title="National Snow and Ice Data Center">National Snow and Ice Data Center</a> (NSIDC) uses satellite data to provide a daily record of Arctic sea ice cover and the rate of melting compared to an average period and specific past years. The Arctic may become ice free for the first time in human history within a few years or by 2040.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>For much of <a href="/wiki/History_of_Europe" title="History of Europe">European history</a>, the north <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_region" title="Polar region">polar regions</a> remained largely unexplored and their geography conjectural. <a href="/wiki/Pytheas" title="Pytheas">Pytheas</a> of <a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">Massilia</a> recorded an account of a journey northward in 325 BC, to a land he called "<a href="/wiki/Thule" title="Thule">Eschate Thule</a>", where the Sun only set for three hours each day and the water was replaced by a congealed substance "on which one can neither walk nor sail". He was probably describing loose sea ice known today as "<a href="/wiki/Iceberg" title="Iceberg">growlers</a>" or "bergy bits"; his "Thule" was probably <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, though the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a> or <a href="/wiki/Shetland" title="Shetland">Shetland</a> have also been suggested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_the_Arctic,_1780s_-_B%26W.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="9392" data-file-width="10928" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Map_of_the_Arctic%2C_1780s_-_B%26W.jpeg/220px-Map_of_the_Arctic%2C_1780s_-_B%26W.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Map_of_the_Arctic%2C_1780s_-_B%26W.jpeg/330px-Map_of_the_Arctic%2C_1780s_-_B%26W.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Map_of_the_Arctic%2C_1780s_-_B%26W.jpeg/440px-Map_of_the_Arctic%2C_1780s_-_B%26W.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_the_Arctic,_1780s_-_B%26W.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Emanuel_Bowen" title="Emanuel Bowen">Emanuel Bowen</a>'s 1780s map of the Arctic features a "Northern Ocean".</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Early <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">cartographers</a> were unsure whether to draw the region around the North Pole as land (as in <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Ruysch" title="Johannes Ruysch">Johannes Ruysch</a>'s <a href="/wiki/File:Ruysch_map.jpg" title="File:Ruysch map.jpg">map of 1507</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Gerardus_Mercator" title="Gerardus Mercator">Gerardus Mercator</a>'s <a href="/wiki/File:Mercator_Septentrionalium_Terrarum_descriptio.jpg" title="File:Mercator Septentrionalium Terrarum descriptio.jpg">map of 1595</a>) or water (as with <a href="/wiki/Martin_Waldseem%C3%BCller" title="Martin Waldseemüller">Martin Waldseemüller</a>'s <a href="/wiki/File:UniversalisCosmographia.jpg" title="File:UniversalisCosmographia.jpg">world map of 1507</a>). The fervent desire of European merchants for a northern passage, the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Sea_Route" title="Northern Sea Route">Northern Sea Route</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Passage" title="Northwest Passage">Northwest Passage</a>, to "<a href="/wiki/Cathay" title="Cathay">Cathay</a>" (<a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>) caused water to win out, and by 1723 mapmakers such as <a href="/wiki/Johann_Homann" title="Johann Homann">Johann Homann</a> featured an extensive "Oceanus Septentrionalis" at the northern edge of their charts.</p>
<p>The few expeditions to penetrate much beyond the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Circle" title="Arctic Circle">Arctic Circle</a> in this era added only small islands, such as <a href="/wiki/Novaya_Zemlya" title="Novaya Zemlya">Novaya Zemlya</a> (11th century) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spitzbergen" title="Spitzbergen">Spitzbergen</a> (1596), though since these were often surrounded by <a href="/wiki/Drift_ice" title="Drift ice">pack-ice</a>, their northern limits were not so clear. The makers of <a href="/wiki/Nautical_chart" title="Nautical chart">navigational charts</a>, more conservative than some of the more fanciful cartographers, tended to leave the region blank, with only fragments of known coastline sketched in.</p>
<p>This lack of knowledge of what lay north of the shifting barrier of ice gave rise to a number of conjectures. In England and other European nations, the <a href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">myth</a> of an "<a href="/wiki/Open_Polar_Sea" title="Open Polar Sea">Open Polar Sea</a>" was persistent. <a href="/wiki/Sir_John_Barrow,_1st_Baronet" title="Sir John Barrow, 1st Baronet">John Barrow</a>, longtime Second Secretary of the British <a href="/wiki/Admiralty" title="Admiralty">Admiralty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arctic_exploration" title="Arctic exploration">promoted exploration</a> of the region from 1818 to 1845 in search of this.</p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> in the 1850s and 1860s, the explorers <a href="/wiki/Elisha_Kane" title="Elisha Kane">Elisha Kane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Israel_Hayes" title="Isaac Israel Hayes">Isaac Israel Hayes</a> both claimed to have seen part of this elusive body of water. Even quite late in the century, the eminent authority <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Fontaine_Maury" title="Matthew Fontaine Maury">Matthew Fontaine Maury</a> included a description of the Open Polar Sea in his textbook <i>The Physical Geography of the Sea</i> (1883). Nevertheless, as all the explorers who travelled closer and closer to the pole reported, the <a href="/wiki/Polar_ice_cap" title="Polar ice cap">polar ice cap</a> is quite thick, and persists year-round.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Fridtjof_Nansen" title="Fridtjof Nansen">Fridtjof Nansen</a> was the first to make a <a href="/wiki/Seamanship" title="Seamanship">nautical</a> crossing of the Arctic Ocean, in 1896. The first surface crossing of the ocean was led by <a href="/wiki/Wally_Herbert" title="Wally Herbert">Wally Herbert</a> in 1969, in a <a href="/wiki/Dog_sled" title="Dog sled">dog sled</a> expedition from <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> to <a href="/wiki/Svalbard" title="Svalbard">Svalbard</a>, with air support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The first nautical transit of the north pole was made in 1958 by the submarine <a href="/wiki/USS_Nautilus_(SSN-571)" title="USS Nautilus (SSN-571)">USS Nautilus</a>, and the first surface nautical transit occurred in 1977 by the <a href="/wiki/Icebreaker" title="Icebreaker">icebreaker</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arktika_(1972_nuclear_icebreaker)" title="Arktika (1972 nuclear icebreaker)">NS Arktika</a>.</p>
<p>Since 1937, Soviet and Russian manned <a href="/wiki/Drifting_ice_station" title="Drifting ice station">drifting ice stations</a> have extensively monitored the Arctic Ocean. Scientific settlements were established on the drift ice and carried thousands of kilometres by ice floes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, the European region of the Arctic Ocean was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arctic_Ocean_operations_of_World_War_II" title="Arctic Ocean operations of World War II">heavily contested</a>: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arctic_convoys" title="Arctic convoys">Allied commitment to resupply the Soviet Union via its northern ports</a> was opposed by German naval and air forces.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IBCAO_betamap.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="868" data-file-width="762" height="456" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/IBCAO_betamap.jpg/400px-IBCAO_betamap.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/IBCAO_betamap.jpg/600px-IBCAO_betamap.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d5/IBCAO_betamap.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:IBCAO_betamap.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Bathymetry" title="Bathymetry">bathymetric</a>/<a href="/wiki/Topography" title="Topography">topographic</a> map of the Arctic Ocean and the surrounding lands.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arctic.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="633" data-file-width="505" height="501" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Arctic.svg/400px-Arctic.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Arctic.svg/600px-Arctic.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Arctic.svg/800px-Arctic.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arctic.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic region</a>; of note, the region's southerly border on this map is depicted by a red <a href="/wiki/Contour_line#Temperature_and_related_subjects" title="Contour line">isotherm</a>, with all territory to the north having an average temperature of less than 10 °C (50 °F) in July.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Arctic Ocean occupies a roughly circular basin and covers an area of about 14,056,000 km<sup>2</sup> (5,427,000 sq mi), almost the size of <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oceans_of_the_World_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oceans_of_the_World-9">[9]</a></sup> The coastline is 45,390 km (28,200 mi) long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arctic_Ocean_Fast_Facts_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arctic_Ocean_Fast_Facts-10">[10]</a></sup> It is surrounded by the land masses of Eurasia, North America, <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>, and by <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Arctic_Islands_(disambiguation)" title="Arctic Islands (disambiguation)">several islands</a>.</p>
<p>It is generally taken to include <a href="/wiki/Baffin_Bay" title="Baffin Bay">Baffin Bay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barents_Sea" title="Barents Sea">Barents Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beaufort_Sea" title="Beaufort Sea">Beaufort Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chukchi_Sea" title="Chukchi Sea">Chukchi Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Siberian_Sea" title="East Siberian Sea">East Siberian Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greenland_Sea" title="Greenland Sea">Greenland Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Bay" title="Hudson Bay">Hudson Bay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Strait" title="Hudson Strait">Hudson Strait</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kara_Sea" title="Kara Sea">Kara Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laptev_Sea" title="Laptev Sea">Laptev Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/White_Sea" title="White Sea">White Sea</a> and other tributary bodies of water. It is connected to the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Bering_Strait" title="Bering Strait">Bering Strait</a> and to the Atlantic Ocean through the <a href="/wiki/Greenland_Sea" title="Greenland Sea">Greenland Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Labrador_Sea" title="Labrador Sea">Labrador Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pidwirny_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pidwirny-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Countries bordering the Arctic Ocean are: Russia, Norway, Iceland, Greenland, Canada and the United States.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extent_and_major_ports">Extent and major ports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Extent and major ports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Borders_of_the_oceans#Arctic_Ocean" title="Borders of the oceans">Borders of the oceans § Arctic Ocean</a></div>
<p>There are several ports and <a href="/wiki/Harbor" title="Harbor">harbours</a> around the Arctic Ocean<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Alaska, the main ports are <a href="/wiki/Barrow,_Alaska" title="Barrow, Alaska">Barrow</a> (<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=71_17_44_N_156_45_59_W_type:city_region:US&amp;title=Barrow"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">71°17′44″N</span> <span class="longitude">156°45′59″W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">71.29556°N 156.76639°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">71.29556; -156.76639</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Barrow</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>) and <a href="/wiki/Prudhoe_Bay,_Alaska" title="Prudhoe Bay, Alaska">Prudhoe Bay</a> (<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=70_19_32_N_148_42_41_W_type:city_region:US&amp;title=Prudhoe"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">70°19′32″N</span> <span class="longitude">148°42′41″W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">70.32556°N 148.71139°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">70.32556; -148.71139</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Prudhoe</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>).</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, ships may anchor at <a href="/wiki/Churchill,_Manitoba" title="Churchill, Manitoba">Churchill</a> (<a href="/wiki/Port_of_Churchill" title="Port of Churchill">Port of Churchill</a>) (<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=58_46_28_N_094_11_37_W_type:city_region:CA-MB&amp;title=Port+of+Churchill"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">58°46′28″N</span> <span class="longitude">094°11′37″W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">58.77444°N 94.19361°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">58.77444; -94.19361</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Port of Churchill</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>) in <a href="/wiki/Manitoba" title="Manitoba">Manitoba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nanisivik" title="Nanisivik">Nanisivik</a> (<a href="/wiki/Nanisivik_Naval_Facility" title="Nanisivik Naval Facility">Nanisivik Naval Facility</a>) (<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=73_04_08_N_084_32_57_W_type:city_region:CA-MB&amp;title=Nanisivik+Naval+Facility"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">73°04′08″N</span> <span class="longitude">084°32′57″W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">73.06889°N 84.54917°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">73.06889; -84.54917</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Nanisivik Naval Facility</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>) in <a href="/wiki/Nunavut" title="Nunavut">Nunavut</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tuktoyaktuk" title="Tuktoyaktuk">Tuktoyaktuk</a> (<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=69_26_34_N_133_01_52_W_&amp;title=Tuktoyaktuk"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">69°26′34″N</span> <span class="longitude">133°01′52″W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">69.44278°N 133.03111°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">69.44278; -133.03111</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Tuktoyaktuk</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>) or <a href="/wiki/Inuvik" title="Inuvik">Inuvik</a> (<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=68_21_42_N_133_43_50_W_type:city_region:CA-NT&amp;title=Inuvik"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">68°21′42″N</span> <span class="longitude">133°43′50″W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">68.36167°N 133.73056°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">68.36167; -133.73056</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Inuvik</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>) in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northwest_territories" title="Northwest territories">Northwest territories</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Greenland">Greenland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Greenland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Greenland, the main port is at <a href="/wiki/Nuuk" title="Nuuk">Nuuk</a> (<a href="/wiki/Nuuk_Port_and_Harbour" title="Nuuk Port and Harbour">Nuuk Port and Harbour</a>) (<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=64_10_15_N_051_43_15_W_type:city_region:GL&amp;title=Nuuk+Port+and+Harbour"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">64°10′15″N</span> <span class="longitude">051°43′15″W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">64.17083°N 51.72083°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">64.17083; -51.72083</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Nuuk Port and Harbour</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>).</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Norway">Norway</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Norway">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Norway, <a href="/wiki/Kirkenes" title="Kirkenes">Kirkenes</a> (<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=69_43_37_N_030_02_44_E_region:NO_type:city&amp;title=Kirkenes"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">69°43′37″N</span> <span class="longitude">030°02′44″E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">69.72694°N 30.04556°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">69.72694; 30.04556</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Kirkenes</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>) and <a href="/wiki/Vard%C3%B8" title="Vardø">Vardø</a> (<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=70_22_14_N_031_06_27_E_region:NO_type:city&amp;title=Vard%C3%B8"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">70°22′14″N</span> <span class="longitude">031°06′27″E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">70.37056°N 31.10750°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">70.37056; 31.10750</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Vardø</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>) are ports on the mainland. Also, there is <a href="/wiki/Longyearbyen" title="Longyearbyen">Longyearbyen</a> (<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=78_13_12_N_15_39_00_E_region:NO_type:city&amp;title=Longyearbyen"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">78°13′12″N</span> <span class="longitude">15°39′00″E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">78.22000°N 15.65000°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">78.22000; 15.65000</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Longyearbyen</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>) on the island of Svalbard next to <a href="/wiki/Fram_Strait" title="Fram Strait">Fram Strait</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Russia">Russia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Russia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Russia, major ports sorted by the different sea areas are:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Murmansk" title="Murmansk">Murmansk</a> (<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=68_58_N_033_05_E_type:city_region:RU&amp;title=Murmansk"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">68°58′N</span> <span class="longitude">033°05′E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">68.967°N 33.083°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">68.967; 33.083</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Murmansk</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>) in the Barents Sea</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Arkhangelsk" title="Arkhangelsk">Arkhangelsk</a> (<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=64_32_N_040_32_E_type:city_region:RU&amp;title=Arkhangelsk"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">64°32′N</span> <span class="longitude">040°32′E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">64.533°N 40.533°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">64.533; 40.533</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Arkhangelsk</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>) in the White Sea</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Labytnangi" title="Labytnangi">Labytnangi</a> (<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=66_39_26_N_066_25_06_E_type:city_region:RU&amp;title=Labytnangi"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">66°39′26″N</span> <span class="longitude">066°25′06″E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">66.65722°N 66.41833°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">66.65722; 66.41833</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Labytnangi</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>) <a href="/wiki/Salekhard" title="Salekhard">Salekhard</a> (<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=66_32_N_066_36_E_type:city_region:RU&amp;title=Salekhard"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">66°32′N</span> <span class="longitude">066°36′E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">66.533°N 66.600°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">66.533; 86.183</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Dudinka</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>), <a href="/wiki/Igarka" title="Igarka">Igarka</a> (<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=67_28_N_86_35_E_type:city_region:RU&amp;title=Igarka"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">67°28′N</span> <span class="longitude">86°35′E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">67.467°N 86.583°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">67.467; <li><a href="/wiki/Tiksi" title="Tiksi">Tiksi</a> (<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=71_38_N_128_52_E_type:city_region:RU&amp;title=Tiksi%2C+city+of+the+Polar+Heros"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">71°38′N</span> <span class="longitude">128°52′E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">71.633°N 128.867°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">71.633; 128.867</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Tiksi, city of the Polar Heros</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>) in the Laptev Sea</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pevek" title="Pevek">Pevek</a> (<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=69_42_N_170_17_E_type:city_region:RU&amp;title=Pevek"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">69°42′N</span> <span class="longitude">170°17′E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">69.700°N 170.283°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">69.700; 170.283</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Pevek</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>) in the East Siberian Sea</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arctic_shelves"><span id="Arctic_shelf"></span>Arctic shelves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Arctic shelves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The ocean's Arctic shelf comprises a number of <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">continental shelves</a>, including the Canadian Arctic shelf, underlying the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Arctic_Archipelago" title="Canadian Arctic Archipelago">Canadian Arctic Archipelago</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf_of_Russia" title="Continental shelf of Russia">Russian continental shelf</a>, which is sometimes simply called the "Arctic Shelf" because it is greater in extent. The Russian continental shelf consists of three separate, smaller shelves, the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Barents_Shelf&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Barents Shelf (page does not exist)">Barents Shelf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chukchi_Sea_Shelf" title="Chukchi Sea Shelf">Chukchi Sea Shelf</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siberian_Shelf" title="Siberian Shelf">Siberian Shelf</a>. Of these three, the Siberian Shelf is the largest such shelf in the world. The Siberian Shelf holds large oil and gas reserves, and the Chukchi shelf forms the border between Russian and the United States as stated in the <a href="/wiki/USSR%E2%80%93USA_Maritime_Boundary_Agreement" title="USSR–USA Maritime Boundary Agreement">USSR–USA Maritime Boundary Agreement</a>. The whole area is subject to international <a href="/wiki/Territorial_claims_in_the_Arctic" title="Territorial claims in the Arctic">territorial claims</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Underwater_features">Underwater features</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Underwater features">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An <a href="/wiki/Mid-ocean_ridge" title="Mid-ocean ridge">underwater ridge</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Lomonosov_Ridge" title="Lomonosov Ridge">Lomonosov Ridge</a>, divides the deep sea <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Basin" title="Arctic Basin">North Polar Basin</a> into two <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_basin" title="Oceanic basin">oceanic basins</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Basin" title="Eurasian Basin">Eurasian Basin</a>, which is between 4,000 and 4,500 m (13,100 and 14,800 ft) deep, and the <a href="/wiki/Amerasian_Basin" title="Amerasian Basin">Amerasian Basin</a> (sometimes called the North American, or Hyperborean Basin), which is about 4,000 m (13,000 ft) deep. The <a href="/wiki/Bathymetry" title="Bathymetry">bathymetry</a> of the ocean bottom is marked by <a href="/wiki/Fault_block" title="Fault block">fault block</a> ridges, <a href="/wiki/Abyssal_plain" title="Abyssal plain">abyssal plains</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_trench" title="Oceanic trench">ocean deeps</a>, and basins. The average depth of the Arctic Ocean is 1,038 m (3,406 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> The deepest point is <a href="/wiki/Litke_Deep" title="Litke Deep">Litke Deep</a> in the Eurasian Basin, at 5,450 m (17,880 ft).</p>
<p>The two major basins are further subdivided by ridges into the <a href="/wiki/Canada_Basin" title="Canada Basin">Canada Basin</a> (between Alaska/Canada and the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Ridge" title="Alpha Ridge">Alpha Ridge</a>), <a href="/wiki/Makarov_Basin" title="Makarov Basin">Makarov Basin</a> (between the Alpha and Lomonosov Ridges), <a href="/wiki/Nansen_Basin" title="Nansen Basin">Nansen Basin</a> (between Lomonosov and <a href="/wiki/Gakkel_Ridge" title="Gakkel Ridge">Gakkel</a> ridges), and Nansen Basin (<a href="/wiki/Amundsen_Basin" title="Amundsen Basin">Amundsen Basin</a>) (between the Gakkel Ridge and the continental shelf that includes the <a href="/wiki/Franz_Josef_Land" title="Franz Josef Land">Franz Josef Land</a>).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceanography">Oceanography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Oceanography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_flow">Water flow</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Water flow">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:652px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BrnBld_BeringToFram.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="650" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/BrnBld_BeringToFram.svg/650px-BrnBld_BeringToFram.svg.png" width="650"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">Distribution of the major <a href="/wiki/Water_mass" title="Water mass">water mass</a> in the Arctic Ocean. The section sketches the different water masses along a vertical section from <a href="/wiki/Bering_Strait" title="Bering Strait">Bering Strait</a> over the geographic <a href="/wiki/North_Pole" title="North Pole">North Pole</a> to <a href="/wiki/Fram_Strait" title="Fram Strait">Fram Strait</a>. As the <a href="/wiki/Stratification_(water)" title="Stratification (water)">stratification</a> is stable, deeper water masses are more dense than the layers above.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Temperature_and_salinity_profiles_in_the_Arctic_Ocean.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="191" data-file-width="271" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Temperature_and_salinity_profiles_in_the_Arctic_Ocean.svg/220px-Temperature_and_salinity_profiles_in_the_Arctic_Ocean.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Temperature_and_salinity_profiles_in_the_Arctic_Ocean.svg/330px-Temperature_and_salinity_profiles_in_the_Arctic_Ocean.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Temperature_and_salinity_profiles_in_the_Arctic_Ocean.svg/440px-Temperature_and_salinity_profiles_in_the_Arctic_Ocean.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Temperature_and_salinity_profiles_in_the_Arctic_Ocean.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Density structure of the upper 1,200 m (3,900 ft) in the Arctic Ocean. Profiles of temperature and salinity for the Amundsen Basin, the Canadian Basin and the Greenland Sea are sketched in this cartoon.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In large parts of the Arctic Ocean, the top layer (about 50 m (160 ft)) is of lower salinity and lower temperature than the rest. It remains relatively stable, because the salinity effect on density is bigger than the temperature effect. It is fed by the freshwater input of the big Siberian and Canadian streams (<a href="/wiki/Ob_River" title="Ob River">Ob</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yenisei_River" title="Yenisei River">Yenisei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lena_River" title="Lena River">Lena</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mackenzie_River" title="Mackenzie River">Mackenzie</a>), the water of which quasi floats on the saltier, denser, deeper ocean water. Between this lower salinity layer and the bulk of the ocean lies the so-called <a href="/wiki/Halocline" title="Halocline">halocline</a>, in which both salinity and temperature are rising with increasing depth.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Copepodkils.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="2000" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Copepodkils.jpg/250px-Copepodkils.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Copepodkils.jpg/375px-Copepodkils.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Copepodkils.jpg/500px-Copepodkils.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Copepodkils.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Copepod" title="Copepod">Copepod</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Because of its relative isolation from other oceans, the Arctic Ocean has a uniquely complex system of water flow. It is classified as a mediterranean sea, which as “a part of the world ocean which has only limited communication with the major ocean basins (these being the Pacific, Atlantic, and Indian Oceans) and where the circulation is dominated by thermohaline forcing”.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tomczak_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tomczak-14">[14]</a></sup> The Arctic Ocean has a total volume of 18.07×10<sup>6</sup> km<sup>3</sup>, equal to about 1.3% of the World Ocean. Mean surface circulation is predominately cyclonic on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eurasian" title="Eurasian">Eurasian</a> side and anticyclonic in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canadian_Basin" title="Canadian Basin">Canadian Basin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Talley_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Talley-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Water enters from both the Pacific and Atlantic Oceans and can be divided into three unique water masses. The deepest water mass is called Arctic Bottom Water and begins around 900 metres (3,000 feet) depth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tomczak_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tomczak-14">[14]</a></sup> It is composed of the densest water in the World Ocean and has two main sources: Arctic shelf water and Greenland Sea Deep Water. Water in the shelf region that begins as inflow from the Pacific passes through the narrow <a href="/wiki/Bering_Strait" title="Bering Strait">Bering Strait</a> at an average rate of 0.8 Sverdrups and reaches the <a href="/wiki/Chukchi_Sea" title="Chukchi Sea">Chukchi Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nature-16">[16]</a></sup> During the winter, cold Alaskan winds blow over the Chukchi Sea, freezing the surface water and pushing this newly formed ice out to the Pacific. The speed of the ice drift is roughly 1–4 cm/s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Talley_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Talley-15">[15]</a></sup> This process leaves dense, salty waters in the sea that sink over the continental shelf into the western Arctic Ocean and create a <a href="/wiki/Halocline" title="Halocline">halocline</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polar_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Polar-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nares_strait_border_(Kennedy_channel).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1249" data-file-width="1765" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Nares_strait_border_%28Kennedy_channel%29.png/220px-Nares_strait_border_%28Kennedy_channel%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Nares_strait_border_%28Kennedy_channel%29.png/330px-Nares_strait_border_%28Kennedy_channel%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Nares_strait_border_%28Kennedy_channel%29.png/440px-Nares_strait_border_%28Kennedy_channel%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nares_strait_border_(Kennedy_channel).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Channel" title="Kennedy Channel">Kennedy Channel</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>This water is met by Greenland Sea Deep Water, which forms during the passage of winter storms. As temperatures cool dramatically in the winter, ice forms and intense vertical convection allows the water to become dense enough to sink below the warm saline water below.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tomczak_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tomczak-14">[14]</a></sup> Arctic Bottom Water is critically important because of its outflow, which contributes to the formation of Atlantic Deep Water. The overturning of this water plays a key role in global circulation and the moderation of climate.</p>
<p>In the depth range of 150–900 metres (490–2,950 feet) is a water mass referred to as Atlantic Water. Inflow from the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Current" title="North Atlantic Current">North Atlantic Current</a> enters through the <a href="/wiki/Fram_Strait" title="Fram Strait">Fram Strait</a>, cooling and sinking to form the deepest layer of the halocline, where it circles the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Basin" title="Arctic Basin">Arctic Basin</a> counter-clockwise. This is the highest volumetric inflow to the Arctic Ocean, equalling about 10 times that of the Pacific inflow, and it creates the Arctic Ocean Boundary Current.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nature-16">[16]</a></sup> It flows slowly, at about 0.02 m/s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tomczak_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-Tomczak-14">[14]</a></sup> Atlantic Water has the same salinity as Arctic Bottom Water but is much warmer (up to 3 °C). In fact, this water mass is actually warmer than the surface water, and remains submerged only due to the role of salinity in density.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tomczak_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-Tomczak-14">[14]</a></sup> When water reaches the basin it is pushed by strong winds into a large circular current called the <a href="/wiki/Beaufort_Gyre" title="Beaufort Gyre">Beaufort Gyre</a>. Water in the Beaufort Gyre is far less saline than that of the Chukchi Sea due to inflow from large Canadian and Siberian rivers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polar_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Polar-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>The final defined water mass in the Arctic Ocean is called Arctic Surface Water and is found from 150–200 metres (490–660 feet). The most important feature of this water mass is a section referred to as the sub-surface layer. It is a product of Atlantic water that enters through canyons and is subjected to intense mixing on the <a href="/wiki/Siberian_Shelf" title="Siberian Shelf">Siberian Shelf</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tomczak_14-5"><a href="#cite_note-Tomczak-14">[14]</a></sup> As it is entrained, it cools and acts a heat shield for the surface layer. This insulation keeps the warm Atlantic Water from melting the surface ice. Additionally, this water forms the swiftest currents of the Arctic, with speed of around 0.3-0.6 m/s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tomczak_14-6"><a href="#cite_note-Tomczak-14">[14]</a></sup> Complementing the water from the canyons, some Pacific water that does not sink to the shelf region after passing through the Bering Strait also contributes to this water mass.</p>
<p>Waters originating in the Pacific and Atlantic both exit through the Fram Strait between <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Svalbard_Island" title="Svalbard Island">Svalbard Island</a>, which is about 2,700 metres (8,900 feet) deep and 350 kilometres (220 miles) wide. This outflow is about 9 Sv.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nature-16">[16]</a></sup> The width of the Fram Strait is what allows for both inflow and outflow on the Atlantic side of the Arctic Ocean. Because of this, it is influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Coriolis_force" title="Coriolis force">Coriolis force</a>, which concentrates outflow to the East Greenland Current on the western side and inflow to the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Current" title="Norwegian Current">Norwegian Current</a> on the eastern side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tomczak_14-7"><a href="#cite_note-Tomczak-14">[14]</a></sup> Pacific water also exits along the west coast of Greenland and the <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Strait" title="Hudson Strait">Hudson Strait</a> (1-2 Sv), providing nutrients to the Canadian Archipelago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-Nature-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>As noted, the process of ice formation and movement is a key driver in Arctic Ocean circulation and the formation of water masses. With this dependence, the Arctic Ocean experiences variations due to seasonal changes in sea ice cover. Sea ice movement is the result of wind forcing, which is related to a number of meteorological conditions that the Arctic experiences throughout the year. For example, the Beaufort High—an extension of the <a href="/wiki/Siberian_High" title="Siberian High">Siberian High</a> system—is a pressure system that drives the anticyclonic motion of the Beaufort Gyre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Talley_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Talley-15">[15]</a></sup> During the summer, this area of high pressure is pushed out closer to its Siberian and Canadian sides. In addition, there is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_level_pressure" title="Sea level pressure">sea level pressure</a> (SLP) ridge over Greenland that drives strong northerly winds through the Fram Strait, facilitating ice export. In the summer, the SLP contrast is smaller, producing weaker winds. A final example of seasonal pressure system movement is the low pressure system that exists over the Nordic and Barents Seas. It is an extension of the <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Low" title="Icelandic Low">Icelandic Low</a>, which creates cyclonic ocean circulation in this area. The low shifts to centre over the North Pole in the summer. These variations in the Arctic all contribute to ice drift reaching its weakest point during the summer months. There is also evidence that the drift is associated with the phase of the Arctic Oscillation and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlantic_Multidecadal_Oscillation" title="Atlantic Multidecadal Oscillation">Atlantic Multidecadal Oscillation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Talley_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-Talley-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sea_ice">Sea ice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Sea ice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="451" data-file-width="540" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg/300px-2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg/450px-2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f0/2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sea cover in the Arctic Ocean, showing the median, 2005 and 2007 coverage <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Arctic_ice_pack" title="Arctic ice pack">Arctic ice pack</a></div>
<p>Much of the Arctic Ocean is covered by sea ice that varies in extent and thickness seasonally. The mean extent of the ice has been decreasing since 1980 from the average winter value of 15,600,000 km<sup>2</sup> (6,023,200 sq mi) at a rate of 3% per decade. The seasonal variations are about 7,000,000 km<sup>2</sup> (2,702,700 sq mi) with the maximum in April and minimum in September. The sea ice is affected by wind and ocean currents, which can move and rotate very large areas of ice. Zones of compression also arise, where the ice piles up to form pack ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buixade_Farre_2014_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buixade_Farre_2014-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Icebergs occasionally break away from northern <a href="/wiki/Ellesmere_Island" title="Ellesmere Island">Ellesmere Island</a>, and icebergs are formed from <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciers</a> in western Greenland and extreme northeastern Canada. These icebergs pose a hazard to ships, of which the <a href="/wiki/RMS_Titanic" title="RMS Titanic"><i>Titanic</i></a> is one of the most famous. <a href="/wiki/Permafrost" title="Permafrost">Permafrost</a> is found on most islands. The ocean is virtually icelocked from October to June, and the <a href="/wiki/Superstructure" title="Superstructure">superstructure</a> of ships are subject to <a href="/wiki/Icing_(nautical)" title="Icing (nautical)">icing</a> from October to May.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-11">[11]</a></sup> Before the advent of modern <a href="/wiki/Icebreaker" title="Icebreaker">icebreakers</a>, ships sailing the Arctic Ocean risked being trapped or crushed by sea ice (although the <i><a href="/wiki/SS_Baychimo" title="SS Baychimo">Baychimo</a></i> drifted through the Arctic Ocean untended for decades despite these hazards).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Climate_change_in_the_Arctic" title="Climate change in the Arctic">Climate change in the Arctic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;">
<div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:210px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:320px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/North_Pole_Sea_Ice_1990-1999.ogg/320px--North_Pole_Sea_Ice_1990-1999.ogg.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:320px;height:240px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="118.83333333333" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="North_Pole_Sea_Ice_1990-1999.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/98/North_Pole_Sea_Ice_1990-1999.ogg" type="video/ogg; codecs=&amp;quot;theora&amp;quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file, 320 × 240 (626 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="320" data-height="240" data-bandwidth="625511" data-framerate="30"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/9/98/North_Pole_Sea_Ice_1990-1999.ogg/North_Pole_Sea_Ice_1990-1999.ogg.160p.ogv" type="video/ogg; codecs=&amp;quot;theora, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Low bandwidth Ogg video (160P)" data-shorttitle="Ogg 160P" data-transcodekey="160p.ogv" data-width="214" data-height="160" data-bandwidth="160432" data-framerate="15"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;'><img alt="File:North Pole Sea Ice 1990-1999.ogg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/North_Pole_Sea_Ice_1990-1999.ogg/210px--North_Pole_Sea_Ice_1990-1999.ogg.jpg" style="width:210px;height:158px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/98/North_Pole_Sea_Ice_1990-1999.ogg" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:North_Pole_Sea_Ice_1990-1999.ogg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Changes in ice between 1990–1999</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Under the influence of the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_glaciation" title="Quaternary glaciation">Quaternary glaciation</a>, the Arctic Ocean is contained in a <a href="/wiki/Polar_climate" title="Polar climate">polar climate</a> characterized by persistent cold and relatively narrow annual temperature ranges. Winters are characterized by the <a href="/wiki/Polar_night" title="Polar night">polar night</a>, extreme cold, frequent low-level temperature inversions, and stable weather conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cyclone" title="Cyclone">Cyclones</a> are only common on the Atlantic side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-23">[23]</a></sup> Summers are characterized by continuous <a href="/wiki/Daylight" title="Daylight">daylight</a> (<a href="/wiki/Midnight_sun" title="Midnight sun">midnight sun</a>), and temperatures can rise above the melting point (0 °C (32 °F). Cyclones are more frequent in summer and may bring rain or snow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-23">[23]</a></sup> It is cloudy year-round, with mean cloud cover ranging from 60% in winter to over 80% in summer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>The temperature of the surface of the Arctic Ocean is fairly constant, near the <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">freezing point</a> of <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">seawater</a>. Because the Arctic Ocean consists of saltwater, the temperature must reach −1.8 °C (28.8 °F) before freezing occurs.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> of sea water, in contrast to fresh water, increases as it nears the freezing point and thus it tends to sink. It is generally necessary that the upper 100–150 m (330–490 ft) of ocean water cools to the freezing point for sea ice to form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> In the winter the relatively warm ocean water exerts a moderating influence, even when covered by ice. This is one reason why the Arctic does not experience the extreme temperatures seen on the <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctic continent</a>.</p>
<p>There is considerable seasonal variation in how much pack ice of the Arctic ice pack covers the Arctic Ocean. Much of the Arctic ice pack is also covered in snow for about 10 months of the year. The maximum snow cover is in March or April — about 20 to 50 cm (7.9 to 19.7 in) over the frozen ocean.</p>
<p>The climate of the Arctic region has varied significantly in the past. As recently as 55 million years ago, during the <a href="/wiki/Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum" title="Paleocene–Eocene Thermal Maximum">Paleocene–Eocene Thermal Maximum</a>, the region reached an average annual temperature of 10–20 °C (50–68 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shellito2003_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shellito2003-26">[26]</a></sup> The surface waters of the northernmost<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Arctic Ocean warmed, seasonally at least, enough to support tropical lifeforms<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> requiring surface temperatures of over 22 °C (72 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sluijs2006_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sluijs2006-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Animal_and_plant_life">Animal and plant life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Animal and plant life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1575" data-file-width="2100" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg/220px-Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg/330px-Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg/440px-Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Three <a href="/wiki/Polar_bear" title="Polar bear">polar bears</a> approach <a href="/wiki/USS_Honolulu_(SSN-718)" title="USS Honolulu (SSN-718)">USS <i>Honolulu</i></a> near the <a href="/wiki/North_Pole" title="North Pole">North Pole</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Endangered marine species in the Arctic Ocean include <a href="/wiki/Walrus" title="Walrus">walruses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Whale" title="Whale">whales</a>. The area has a fragile <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a> which is slow to change and slow to recover from disruptions or damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-11">[11]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lion%27s_mane_jellyfish" title="Lion's mane jellyfish">Lion's mane jellyfish</a> are abundant in the waters of the Arctic, and the <a href="/wiki/Banded_gunnel" title="Banded gunnel">banded gunnel</a> is the only species of <a href="/wiki/Pholidae" title="Pholidae">gunnel</a> that lives in the ocean.</p>
<p>The Arctic Ocean has relatively little plant life except for <a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">phytoplankton</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Phytoplankton are a crucial part of the ocean and there are massive amounts of them in the Arctic, where they feed on nutrients from rivers and the <a href="/wiki/Ocean_current" title="Ocean current">currents</a> of the Atlantic and Pacific oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAA_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-31">[31]</a></sup> During summer, the sun is out day and night, thus enabling the phytoplankton to <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesize</a> for long periods of time and reproduce quickly. However, the reverse is true in winter when they struggle to get enough light to survive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAA_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_resources">Natural resources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Natural resources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Natural_resources_of_the_Arctic" title="Natural resources of the Arctic">Natural resources of the Arctic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Territorial_claims_in_the_Arctic" title="Territorial claims in the Arctic">Territorial claims in the Arctic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Marine_mammal" title="Marine mammal">Marine mammal</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">Petroleum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas_field" title="Natural gas field">fields</a>, <a href="/wiki/Placer_deposit" title="Placer deposit">placer deposits</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manganese_nodule" title="Manganese nodule">polymetallic nodules</a>, sand and gravel <a href="/wiki/Construction_aggregate" title="Construction aggregate">aggregates</a>, fish, seals and whales can all be found in abundance in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buixade_Farre_2014_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Buixade_Farre_2014-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>The political dead zone near the centre of the sea is also the focus of a mounting dispute between the United States, Russia, Canada, Norway, and Denmark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> It is significant for the global <a href="/wiki/Energy_market" title="Energy market">energy market</a> because it may hold 25% or more of the world's undiscovered oil and gas resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_concerns">Environmental concerns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Environmental concerns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Climate_change_in_the_Arctic" title="Climate change in the Arctic">Climate change in the Arctic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ozone_depletion" title="Ozone depletion">Ozone depletion</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pollution_in_the_Arctic_Ocean" title="Pollution in the Arctic Ocean">Pollution in the Arctic Ocean</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arctic_ice_melting">Arctic ice melting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Arctic ice melting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Arctic ice pack is thinning, and in many years there is also a seasonal hole in the <a href="/wiki/Ozone_layer" title="Ozone layer">ozone layer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Reduction of the area of Arctic sea ice reduces the planet's average <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a>, possibly resulting in <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> in a positive feedback mechanism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buixade_Farre_2014_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-Buixade_Farre_2014-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R.Black_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-R.Black-35">[35]</a></sup> Research shows that the Arctic may become ice free for the first time in human history within a few years or by 2040.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsidc2011_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-nsidc2011-37">[37]</a></sup> Estimates vary for when the last time the Arctic was ice free: 65 million years ago when fossils indicate that plants existed there to as few as 5,500 years ago; ice and ocean cores going back 8000 years to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holocene_thermal_maximum" title="Holocene thermal maximum">last warm period</a> or 125,000 during the <a href="/wiki/Eemian" title="Eemian">last intraglacial period</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nsidc_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nsidc-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>Warming temperatures in the Arctic may cause large amounts of fresh <a href="/wiki/Meltwater" title="Meltwater">meltwater</a> to enter the north Atlantic, possibly disrupting global <a href="/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation" title="Thermohaline circulation">ocean current patterns</a>. Potentially severe changes in the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a> might then ensue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R.Black_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-R.Black-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>As the extent of sea ice diminishes and sea level rises, the effect of storms such as the <a href="/wiki/Great_Arctic_Cyclone_of_2012" title="Great Arctic Cyclone of 2012">Great Arctic Cyclone of 2012</a> on open water increases, as does possible salt-water damage to vegetation on shore at locations such as the Mackenzie's <a href="/wiki/River_delta" title="River delta">river delta</a> as stronger <a href="/wiki/Storm_surge" title="Storm surge">storm surges</a> become more likely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CC03513_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-CC03513-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Clathrate_breakdown">Clathrate breakdown</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Clathrate breakdown">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Clathrate_gun_hypothesis" title="Clathrate gun hypothesis">Clathrate gun hypothesis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright" style=";">
<div class="thumbinner" style="overflow:hidden;width:332px;">

</div>
<div style="visibility:hidden">
<div class="noresize"><a href="/wiki/File:Extinction_intensity.svg" title="File:Extinction intensity.svg"><img alt="Extinction intensity.svg" data-file-height="324" data-file-width="531" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Extinction_intensity.svg/320px-Extinction_intensity.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Extinction_intensity.svg/480px-Extinction_intensity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Extinction_intensity.svg/640px-Extinction_intensity.svg.png 2x" usemap="#ImageMap_1_1496920919" width="320"/></a><map id="ImageMap_1_1496920919" name="ImageMap_1_1496920919">

<area alt="Neogene" coords="304,0,316,14" href="/wiki/Neogene" shape="rect" title="Neogene"></area></map></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">The Permian–Triassic extinction event (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Dying" title="Great Dying">Great Dying</a>) may have been caused by release of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clathrate" title="Clathrate">clathrates</a>. An estimated 52% of marine <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> became extinct, representing 96% of all marine <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sea_ice" title="Sea ice">Sea ice</a>, and the cold conditions it sustains, serves to stabilise <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> deposits on and near the shoreline,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-independent2008_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-independent2008-40">[40]</a></sup> preventing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clathrate" title="Clathrate">clathrate</a> breaking down and outgassing <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> into the atmosphere, causing further warming. Melting of this ice may release large quantities of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, a powerful <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> into the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>, causing further warming in a strong <a href="/wiki/Positive_feedback" title="Positive feedback">positive feedback</a> cycle and; marine genus and species to become extinct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-independent2008_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-independent2008-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_concerns">Other concerns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Other concerns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Other <a href="/wiki/Environmentalism" title="Environmentalism">environmental concerns</a> relate to the <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_contamination" title="Radioactive contamination">radioactive contamination</a> of the Arctic Ocean from, for example, Russian <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_waste" title="Radioactive waste">radioactive waste</a> dump sites in the Kara Sea<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_testing" title="Nuclear weapons testing">nuclear test sites</a> such as Novaya Zemlya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> In addition, Shell planned to drill exploratory wells in the Chukchi and Beaufort seas during the summer of 2012, which environmental groups filed a lawsuit about in an attempt to protect native communities, endangered wildlife, and the Arctic Ocean in the event of a major oil spill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>On July 16, 2015, five nations (United States of America, Russia, Canada, Norway, Denmark/Greenland) signed a declaration committing to keep their fishing vessels out of a 1.1 million square mile zone in the central Arctic Ocean near the North Pole. The agreement calls for those nations to refrain from fishing there until there is better scientific knowledge about the marine resources and until a regulatory system is in place to protect those resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
